 dangers of ice-covered streets 
The area Ice Plan was instituted last November to help prevent acci­ dents due to icy conditions by ke(*p- ing traffic moving and streets and expressways open. 
City police and public works e m ­ ployes and highw ay d e p a r tm e n t workers were prepared to send sand­ ing crews out and barricade icy lo­ cations as part of the plan 
San Antonio police reported the city experienced 59 auto accidents from 
6:45 a m. to 2:45 p.m. Friday. There were no fatalities recorded during the period, but there w ere 19 injuries recorded, mostly minor. 
Icy conditions, however, contribut­ ed to one traffic death in the San An­ tonio area, investigators said. 
Lewis Updike, 53. Atascosa, was killed early Friday in a one-car acci­ dent near Falls City on U.S. IKL 
Investigators said it appeared Up­ dike lost control of his car on an icy bridge over the San Antonio River in 
Karnes County and overturned. 
Fatality 
It also m arked the first traffic fa­ tality in the area since the long lutli- day weekend traffic count began at ti p.m. Thursday. 
San Antonio recorded its first holi­ day period traffic fatality Thursday night when Paul Ruiz, 64. of 57o IV. 
Mayfield Blvd., was struck by a car as he walked along U.S. 281. 
Police said icy road conditions, caused by the sudden freeze and rain 
Friday morning were the main cause for the large number of accidents. 
Strong winds 
While strong winds reminded San 
Antonians that winter was still with them, local tem peratures climbed to 
41 degrees by 3 p.m. However, the therm om eter dropped five degrees by 4 p.m ., with a prom ise of even lower tem peratures as 1976 came to a close. 
The promise was kept. 
San Antonio wasn’t alone. The rest of Texas welcomed the New Y ear with subfreezing tem peratures and tin* threat, if not actuality, of rain and possible snow flurries. 
T he N ational W e a th er S erv ice forecast predicted chances for snow , sleet or rain all over tlie stu te , in­ cluding the Texas coast. 
Northerly winds accompanying the cold front pushed the chill factor in 
San Antonio down to IO d eg rees. 
Winds were expected lo lessen Sa­ turday,

Texas9 cheapest food in S.A. 

SfECIA l TO IM I EXPRESS* NEW!* 
AUSTIN — For the fourth straight month. San Antonio’s grocery bill of 
$16.94 capped the list us the cheapest of 12 Texas m arket busket survey cities. 
The survey conducted by the Texas 
Departm ent of Agriculture (TDA) found the average statewide price of 
2o com m only pu rch ased grocery items totaled $18.32 in December, 
Total cost was some 13 cents above the average of November but only IO cents more than that reported four months earlier. 
In releasing the December market busket survey of the 12 Texas cities. 

Texas Agriculture loin. .John White noted grocery prices remained rela­ tively stable throughout 197H. 
National reports issued by the U.S. 
Department of Agriculture (USDA) also indicate food prices arc expected to continue on a steady course into the New Year. 
"But while the current food price situation may be stable ut tin* retail level, producers at the beginning of the food chain generally had little to shout about in 1976 and can only look to the new year for any improvement in their profit picture," White said. 
F arm prices for 1976 wound up at 
66 per cent of parity, the lowest 

 recorded iii 43 years, with no crops or livestock except potatoes having gone above effective parity in m onths. 
Rarity is computed as the minimum price at which production costs can be recouped. 
While San Antonio led the survey with the lowest total, some 17 cents above November, the highest total. 
IIH.99 for McAllen / Edinburg, was 36 cents above a month earlier. 
Other cities included in the survey in order of total price and with comparisons to their November sur­ veys are; 
Austin, $17.54, up 13 cents; Laredo, 
$18, down six cents; Tyler. $18.22, up 

 nine cents, E l Rasu, IIH 32. up ll) cents; Abilene, $18 35, down nine cents, and Dallas. $18.42, down three cents 
Also, Houston, $18.59. up one cent; 
Fort Worth. $18.73, up two cents, 
Corpus Christi, $18.74, up 3o cents, and Lubbock, $18.95, up 56 cents

Callaway takes over 

Remember Howard H. C allaway? 
He resigned under fire as Presi­ dent Ford’s secretary of the army after it was alleged he misused his office to obtain a favorable United 
States Forest Service ruling that allowed for expansion of the Crested 
Butte ski area in Colorado, where 
Callaw ay and his b ro th e r had bought a resort development cor­ poration in 197o. 
Now Callaway is pulling up stakes in Georgia and moving to Crested 
Butte to become president and chief executive officer of the corporation. 
He succeeds his brother-in-law, 
Ralph Walton, who succeeds him as board chairman.

Count o f Paris hospitalized 

Henri. Count of Paris, pretender to the French throne, was admitted to a Paris hospital Friday after suffering a heart attack. His condi­ tion was reported as not serious. 
A hospital spokesman said the 
68-year-old count would remain for a few- days under observation. 

The count is a direct descendant of King Louis XIV. In the unlikely event that France were to restore the monarchy, he would be a can­ didate for tile throne. 
The family has been permitted to live in France since 195o after a banishment of 64 years.

In the ensuing years, “ old doubts have remained and new disturbing questions hi)vc tyoen raised as addi­ tional evidence has come to light," it said.

Most of them w ere a rre s te d on drug charges, usually for possessing a few pinches of marijuana, but so­ metimes merely for attending a parly where m arijuana was smoked. Often the evidence-against them was du­ bious. 
The U.S. government has done lit­ tle to get them out. What they may not know is that the U.S. government helped to put them in. 
For Washington officialdom, in its zeal to halt the drug traffic from La­ tin A m erica, brought p ressu re on various governments to crack down on drug violators. Unfortunately, the dragnets caught few sm ugglers but many users, including more than a few innocents. 
We have written in the past about the plight of the young A m ericans who have received stiff sentences in 
Latin America for drug offenses that would have been excused in the 
United States. A couple of sum m ers ago. our associate Joe Spear inter­ viewed several of them in Mexican jails. 
Their arrests were the direct result of pressure from Washington. 
Spotlight 

Today, we turn the spotlight on 
Bolivia, whose harsh narcotics laws failed to distinguish between pushers and users until a few weeks ago. Thus a person caught with a gram of co­ caine could be sentenced to 25 years in miserable Bolivian jails. 
These tough, new narcotics laws were drafted in 1973 by a special Bolivian c o m m is ­ sion. The United States ac­ tually helped to pay the sa­ laries of the Bolivian attor­ neys who were appointed to the commission. The United 
S tates also d isp a tc h e d a 
Justice Department lawyer to Bolivia with a proposed 
111 narcotics code. 
Thereafter, Bolivian and American narcotics agents worked closely to­ gether. This cooperation earned Bo­ livia some generous U.S. financial aid. The United States, for example, is planning to supply the Bolivian police with vehicles, telecommunica­ tions, contract advisers and investi­ gative equipment. 
The United States will also help train the police and will pay part of the operational costs. No less than. 
Secretary of State Henry Kissinger, a fte r m e e tin g with the Bolivian leaders, urged U.S. support of the 
Bolivian narcotics campaign. 
This cam p aig n , m eanw hile, has caught several American youths who swear they are guilty of no more than minor misdemeanors or are innocent altogether. The narcotics penalities w ere so h a rs h , ac c o rd in g to our sources, that Bolivian judges have been unwilling to sentence the young 
Americans to long sentences. 
Legal limbo 
Many A m ericans, therefore, are waiting in legal limbo for hearings. 
Several have joined in sending P re­ sident-elect Jim m y Carter a poignant plea for help, 
“ We arc presumed guilty until we prove o u rse lv e s in n o c e n t,“ they write. “ Some people have not had a hearing in over a year. It seems the 
Bolivian officials do not know what to do with us . . . We are all young . . . some of us with children, and all of us with families we love and m iss.’’ 
Sensitive sources inside Bolivia tell us, for example, that one 21-year-old w om an h as been in jail for six 

Jack 
Anderson 

 m onths. Yet she was detained on purely circumstantial grounds. 
She had been invited to the hotel room of friends to listen to music. 
A ccording to so u rces, the e n tire group was arrested 3o minutes later by Bolivian police, who found 4o grams of cocaine underneath a m at­ tress. One man confessed the cocaine belonged to him alone. The entire group, nevertheless, has been jailed. 
The young woman was sent to a prison-eonvent. 
Each day she is locked in an outside courtyard and then herded inside at night. Tile Bo­ livian go v ern m en t co n trib u tes 12 cents a day to her support. It takes a monthly cheek from her parents to feed her.

COLD 

 just plain ole miserable weather. 
The National Weather Service said the cold weather siege would proba­ bly last through early Monday. 
New Year’s celebrants could thank a massive high pressure system for the virtually nationwide freeze. 
The system delivered frigid air from the Rockies eastward through 
New England, the Appalachians and south to the western Gulf of Mexico. 
The Rio Grande Valley also felt the nip of winter. The weather service’s office in Brownsville reported it an­ ticipated sleet and rain with a possi­ bility of snow for the Valley as a New 
Year’s Day gift.

School Boards will file a mo­ tion for a rehearing in its utili­ ty fight against the city, attor­ ney Harvey Hardy said Friday. 
The Texas Supreme Court Friday rilled against 12 Bexar County School 
Districts in their joint utility suit with Bexar County and the county hospital district against the city and 
City Public Service.

Good year 

By DAVID HENDRICKS 

The year 1977 A.D. is going to be a good one — at least that's the expectation of San 
Antonians. 

"Hopefully, 1977 will bring a new era of goodwill, optimism and pro­ gress for our city," said Mayor Lila 
Cockrell Friday. 

She said she sees growth and de­ velopment as well as improvements of the city’s drainage system and streets as well as some replacement of substandard water mains. 

" I see the continuation of the very warm relations we have established with our friends and neighbors in 
Mexico leading to increased trade, cultural and social exchanges between our countries,” the mayor said. 

 councilmen elected from IO geogra­ phic districts. 
Bexar County Democratic Chair­ man Joyce Peters said she cannot get an accurate reading on the voters’ mood, although she felt some sup­ porters for the districting idea are changing their minds. 
"However, I predict we ll have higher Noter turnouts in 1977 for the 
City Charter and City Council elec­ tions,” she said, explaining, “ The voters will be turned on and come out swinging. They smell blood." 
Mrs. Peters’ Republican counter­ part in Bexar County, Richard 
Bridleman, predicted a year of growth in the Republican Party. 
“ We continue to meet people inter­ ested in building up the two-party system and fiscal responsibility in 
Bexar County and Texas," Eddleman said. 

 the Public Utilities Act excused the school districts from paying the 14 per cent as of Sept. I. 1975," he said. 
School districts have not paid that portion since that time, he explained. 
School, city and county officials were unavailable for comment. 
According to the court’s ruling. San 
Antonio’s city-owned gas and electric system has authority to raise its fuel adjustment costs without City 
Council approval of each increase. 

Courts 

The courts also ruled the portion of city utility charges that goes into the general fund is not an illegal tax on 
Bexar County, the county hospital district and the 12 school districts in the county. 
Fourteen per cent of the utility re­ venues goes into the city treasury and paid for nearly a fourth of the city’s budget in 1974. 
That 14 per cent, the court ruled, is a profit, not a tax. (Since the suit was filed, council has reduced the portion to about ll per cent.) 
With regard to the legality of fuel adjustment costs tacked on to bills without City Council approval, the court said cities are entitled to a reasonable profit on municipal utility systems. 
The 1949 legislature removed a prohibition against such profits for cities such as San Antonio that still owed money to holders of bonds that financed utility construction. 
Profit 
"Since the removal of the restric­ tion. the City of San Antonio has en­ joyed the usual right to make a rea­ sonable profit from its gas and elec­ tric systems even while they are en­ cumbered." the court said. 

As for the fuel adjustment factors, the court ruled mere was no improper delegation of ratemaking authority since City Council gave the 
City Public Service board detailed instructions on how to compute them. 

"No discretion is left by which this computation may be changed," the court said. 

Re-clection 

"And in my crystal ball, I see my re-election,” the mayor said of the 
April City Council elections. "Bu t that prediction relies heavily on con­ tinued help of my friends and sup­ porters in the community." 

About incoming City Mgr. Thomas 
Huebner, the mayor said, " I see the likelihood of improved city services and sound fiscal management with the arrival of the new city manager.” 

Huebner said Friday from his Sa­ cramento, Calif., home he hopes to see the mayor's prediction come true. 

" I t ’s not an easy job, but we’re certainly going to give it a try," 
Huebner said. 

Structure 

" I will probably have established the organizational structure (in city management) that I want to work with by the end of 1977." 

Bexar County Judge Blair Reeves said he could see an upturn in the economy and employment picture of the county and San Antonio as well as downtown being helped with more people living there. 

City Public Service Board of 
Trustees Chairman Tom Berg said he could foresee good news for utility customers. 

‘Tm looking for 1977 to be the first year in many that utility rates will finally stabilize as we bring our large coal fire plants into service and will start a new era of stabilized rates,” 
Berg said. 

He added he wished the city and 
CPS suit against its natural gas sup­ plier, Coastal States CJas Corp., was behind him. 

" I hope 1977 brings a successful settlement out of court,” he said, 
"Stability in gas prices can be a rea­ lity only with a strong gas supplier, not one that is always being threa­ tened with bankruptcy." 

Disagreement between the city and its firemen and policemen are likely' to continue in 1977, said Curtis Franz, president of the San Antonio Fire­ lig h ters Assn., Local 642, 

He noted there will be court action to decide whether the city will pay accumulated sick leave pay as pro­ vided in a new state law. 
Negotiations 

Negotiations between firemen and the city will occur also in 1977, Franz said, adding the outcome will depend on the attitude of the new city manager and the new City Council. 

“ The firemen will take an active, role in city politics,” Franz said, 
"and we hope we will have some impact." 

No one, including Mayor Cockrell, would predict the outcome of the City 
Charter revision election to have

Utility Commission sets hearing on Bell motion 

 s k o a l t o m i u m s v N t w s 

AUSTIN - The Public U tility 
Commission Friday scheduled a Jan. 

HI hearing on Southwestern Bell 
Telephone Co.’s motion for a rehear­ ing on its rate request 

Bell fill'd for a rehearing Tuesday after the PUC cut Bell’s requested rate increase from $298 million to 
$57.8 million. 

Basic service rates in San Antonio have been reduced bv 35 cents, with additional reductions depending on 
.tintype of phone, as a result of the 
PUC’s order. 

PUC Com. Alan Erwin said there was no chance the commission would reconsider raising the $57.8 million rate figure granted. 

“ I still have not heard anything to chango my opinion that ifs a valid order," Erwin said. 
Bell Tuesday charged the commis­ sion “ violated the statutory mandate of the Public Utility Regulatory Act, ignored lls own regulations, denied the company due process of law and confiscated company property." 
PUC’s chief attorney, John Bell. said he disagreed with the telephone company’s charges " I Hunk we’re just fine and we’ve complied with all the laws.” 
Both Erwin and Bell said technical points contained in the order would lie decided during a rehearing. Erwin also said there could tie areas in the present order which need clearing. 
Los Pastores set at mission 

The world’s longest letter to Santa Claus containing 9,6o9 Christmas messages is on its way to 
Middlesex, England, where it will be entered into the Guinness Book of World Records. Texas and 24 foreign nations are represented on the record-setting roll which measures 2,252 feet by 24 inches. 
Linda Senger unrolls it in front of the Main Post Office lust before it was folded and mailed to England.

City attorneys said Friday a continued recess in pretrial hearines of the city’s $472.5 million suit against Coastal 
States Gas Corp. would aid settlement negotiations. 
Jury choice m oves slowly 
* 

TUCSON. Ari/ (A P) - Publicity that ted in the mistrial in Phoenix in an investigative newspaper reporter’s slaying has not affected potential jurors as m u c h iii the second trial in Tucson 
Two more potential jurors were seated in a halfday session Friday, bringing the total chosen in two days to seven. When 37 are selected, the defense and prosecution can each eliminate It), leaving a 12-member panel and five alternates. 

Judge Peter Michael Curry, of ltitith District Court, continued the recess until Jan. IO. The plea of pri­ vilege hearing was scheduled to restart Monday. 
Ed Burke, one of the city’s attor­ neys, said the hearing recess would definitely help the continuing nego­ tiations between the city, City Public 
Service and Coastal States officials move toward settlement. 
Judge Curry said out-of-court settlement was so close as to warrant a week s delay in the hearings. 
Hearings 
The hearings have been in recess since Din- 2o. 
Judge Curry added another week of recess would not only give attorneys more time to work on the settlement, but it would also save money in court costs and attorney fees. 
The hearings are being held to consider Coastal Stales request to move the city/CPS suit against its gas supplier lo the corporation s home city. Houston. 
The suit centers on the city’s gas 

 supply contract an alleged breach of contract by Coastal States of the ty’s gas supply contract. 

Los Pastures, the shepherd’s play, will bt* performed by the 
Guadalupe Church Players on the grounds of the' San Jose 
Mission Saturday and Sunday. 

The play Is sponsored by the San 
Antonio Conservation Society. There is no admission charge. Mass siari?> at ti p.m. with the performance fol­ lowing at 7 p.m. 

Los Bastores was brought to the 
New World by early Spanish mis­ sionaries in an attempt to convert the 
Indians to Christianity. 

Drama 

It is said Father Antonio Margil, who founded Mission San Jose, per­ formed the play for Indians here 225 years ago. 

The drama has been handed down by word of mouth, so many interpre­ tations have evolved, hut the main plot remains essentially unchanged. 

Los Pasiores is the story of she­ pherds who, in the search for the 
Christ Child, are beset by seven de­ vils in fearsome masks who attempt 

 to divert them from their quest.

Nonunion help and a decision by unionized musicians and entertainers to cross the lines assured that the parties would In* held as usual. The musicians approved a lieu Conrad early i i i December 
Maids, food servers, laundry workers, bellhops and bartenders launched their strike on Christmas

Kiev added Miami's Dupont Plaza and Four Ambassadors to the list ol struck hotels. Union officials said additional hotels would In1 struck as the strike continued

We haven’t figured it out. 
All around us we are hear­ ing the fa m ilia r g re etin g : 
Happy New Year! 
From some it has the ring of a g e t-w e ll-c a r d . O thers make it sound like a plaintive hope, as if they w ere really saying: Good luck b e c a u s e you’re going to need it. 
A few give the greeting with a snap-crac kle-pop sureness as if they anticipate 77 will be a year in which things can be done. 
We share the latter a p ­ proach to tile custom. There s logic and history to support that view. 
During 
Bicentennial

Treasury last week $4,327,271.41. 
That m akes Hungary cu r­ rent on its debts to the U.S. and allows American residents to buy Hungarian securities or make loans to Hungarian resi­ dents 
But t h e r e ’s irony in the 
Hungarian condition vis a vis 

 the V. S. The V. S. has made grants, loans and given other assistance to many countries user the years, some of which debt has been forgiven, some still on the books and so m e clearly uncollectable. 
Hungary’s debt is for pur­ chase of surplus flour in the 
U.S. with money from a World 
War I relief loan. That was 75 years ago.

Letter misses point entirely 

Sir: Mr. McMillan’s letter (Dec. 
28) taking Pat Buchanan to task for his suggestion that the GGP exploit the inherent weaknesses of the di­ verse coalition which elected Jim m y 
(’arter, misses the point entirely. 
At issue is not the left-of-center 
Cabinet appointments made by our next President, but the fact that the ethnic voters who put him in office did so in the belief that he was a conservative opposed to more federal interference in their lives. These people were traditional Democrats and Independents who voted Repub­ lican in 1968 and 1972 because of 
Richard Nixon’s conservative im age and were then betrayed by the Nix- on-Ford administration acceptance of the D em ocrat’s Great Society do­ mestic program. 
As Jim m y Carter continues to shed his old conservative image by capi­ tulating to the demands of the De­ mocratic left-wing he will lose the critical support of ethnic voters to the 
Republicans. But this will happen only if the GOF shakes the establish- mentarian image of the Ford-Rocke­ feller wing and adopts the conserva­ tive unti-W ashm gton view uf the 
Reagan-Simon party majority. This is precisely what Buchanan is advo­ cating for the party, a recognition that traditional philosophy is the key to future success.

Dozens better qualified than Bell 

BOSTON 
— 
In 
1966 the 
Georgia House of Representa­ tives refused to seat Julian 
Bond, a newly elected black member. 
The reason was his endorsement of a statement by the Student Non-vio­ lent Coordinating C om m ittee criti­ cizing the war in Vietnam and sym ­ pathizing with men who resisted the draft for service there. 
Bond sued, claiming a violation of his right to free expression under the 
First Amendment . 
He lost in the F ed era l D istric t 
Court. 
An opinion co-authored by Judge 
Griffin Bell said: "The SNCC state­ ment is at war with the national po­ licy of this country. . . . We are com ­ mitted in Vietnam.” 
Legislator 
A legislator lias less right to free speech than a private citizen , the opinion reasoned, because he takes an oath to support the Constitution. 
The court said the Georgia House could properly find Bond’s endorse­ m ent of the 
SNQC sta te m e n t inconsistent with the oath, and could exclude him. 
That decision was quickly and un­ animously reversed by the Supreme 
Court. 
Read today, just IO years later, it seem s like something from the dark a g e s of ju d ic ia l jin g o ism d u rin g 
World War I. 
No one would bother to read such an anacronistic, discredited opinion now — except that it so recently was the considered judgment of the man named to be the next attorney gener­ al of the United States. 
Bell 

J im m y C arter sa y s that Griffin 
Bell is superbly qualified. With most n om in ees, such a c la im would be hard to test. 
But a man who sits on the bench for fifteen years, as Bell did, leaves a record — his opinions in the law* re­ ports. 
I have read a very sm all part of 
Judge Bell’s large output, but I think enough for a fair im pression of his work. 
The Bell opinions I have read are singularly opaque. 
They show none of the intellectual com m an d , the m a rsh a lin g of la n ­ guage that mark a great appellate judge. 
They ring of no passion for justice. 
They are unanalytical, almost inarti­ culate. 
So tar as one can tell the quality of mind behind them — and you usually can tell a good deal — it is second rate. 
C arter, in ad d ition to p r a isin g 
Judge B ell’s intellectual q u alifica­ tions, has implied that lie was a ju­ dicial leader in bringing the South into a new age on race relations. The record is otherwise. 
Record 

The United States Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, on which Judge 
Bell sat, was at the center of the s tr u g g le o ver se g r e g a tio n in th e 
South. But other judges led the fight tor compliance with the Constitution. 
They ar** among our great contem­ porary ju d icial h eroes, and th eir names are no secret: am ong them 
Richard I . Rives, Elbert P. Tuttle and John Minor Wisdom. 
Griffin Bell often disagreed with 
Judges Rives, Tuttle and Wisdom on racial questions. As the S u p rem e 
Court spelled out the law*, clo sin g mort* and more loopholes. Judge Bell wrote opinions complying in narrow terms, 
He by no means defied the law in tile name of segregation, hut it is a travesty to class him with his legen­ dary colleagues on the Fifth Circuit. 
Those im pressions of Griffin Bell as a ludge were checked with people who know the man and his work inti­ mately, 
The unhappy but unavoidable con­ clusion is that he is an u n d is tin ­ guished legal thinker, conservative iii in stin c t, with no d em o n stra ted breadth of m oral or philosophical outlook. 
Criticism 

Much of the criticism lias focused mistakenly on Griffin Bell’s personal friendship with J im m y Carter. A president must have confidence in his attorney general, and friendship need not he disabling. 
The real question is whether the friend has the needed qu alities of mind and heart. 
Herbert Brownell Jr. had the cre­ dentials on paper when P resid ent 
E isen h ow er n a m ed h im , hut he turned out to be an opportunist. 
Robert Kennedy had few on paper, but he had an open mind and a com­ passion that made him a humane in­ fluence on his brother. 
Griffin B d l has a reputation as a 
-killed negotiator. The chances un* thai lie will he a belter attorney gen­ eral than some. 
He is not lik e ly to sink to the corrupt level of, say, Richard Klein- dienst or John Mitchell. 
But will he have the Independence and the moral conviction and the in. tellectual self-con fiden ce to resist p r essu re from the in te llig e n c e agencies as Edward Levi has? Or to hear the tither necessary burdens? 
The record is not encouraging. 
An attorney general of unassailable q u ality is m ore ce n tr a l in th is country now than in the past.

New laws are matters o f life9 death and taxes

Social Security taxes for mil lions rises in r77 

AbSOCiATtD MESS 

WASHINGTON — Millions of 
Americans will pay as much as 
$7o.2o more in Social Security taxes in the new year. 
By law, the m axim um annual tax paym ent in c reases from $895.o5 in 
1976 to $965.25 iii 1977. F orty y e a rs ago, in 1937, the m axim um tax was 
$3o a year. 
But only th e 19 m illio n or so 
Americans earning more than $15,3oo will pay a higher tax in 1977. The tax ra te on a w o rk e r s in c o m e w ill rem ain at 5.85 per cent, so persons who earn less than $15,3oo will not jiay any m ore than they paid on the sam e amount of earnings last year. 
Cutoff raised 
In 1976, a w o rk e r h ad p a id all 
Social Security tax obligations by the tim e he reached the $15,-3oo salary level, and income over this was not subject to additional tax. P ersons for whom tax deductions stopped in 1976 after they reach ed the cutoff point will find the tax is being d ed u cte d , from their checks longer. 
The cutoff for wages and salaries in 
1977 has been raised to $16,5oo, above 

 which no additional Social Security tax will he collected. About 16 million workers earn more than $16,5oo 
The increase in the tax is autom atic in the year after which there has been an increase in Social Security b e n ­ efits, which rose 8.4 per cent i i i 1976. 
The m axim um Social Security tux on self-em ployed p erson s will rise from $1,2o8 7o4o $1,3o3,5o in 1977. 
The in c reases, which h av e to be matched by em ployers, will produce an e s t im a te d $2.3 b illio n fo r th e 
Social Security system. However, this w ill not c lo s e th e g a p of b e n e f i t p a y m e n ts o v e r in c o m e w h ich am ounted to about $4 3 billion lust year. 
Rising sharply 

Although the Social S ecu rity tax bite has been rising sharply since the early 196os. it still has not kept pace with the flow of benefits. T h e re is concern that the s y s te m s re s e rv e s will be eroded and that it may have to turn to general tax revenues to keep gouig. 
The m axim um tax was just $39 a year between 1937 and 1949. Then it rose slowly to $174 in 1963. Additional benefits for M edicare for the aged 

 and disabled pushed the m a x im u m tax to $277.2o by 1966. 
Increases becam e even steeper in subsequent y ears, rising to a m a x ­ im um $29o.4o in 1967, $343.2o in 1968, 
$374 4o in 1969, $4o5.6o in 1971, $468 in 
1972. $631 8o in 1973, $772 2o in 1974, 
$824 85 in 1975 and $895 o5 iii 1976. 
Also in 
1977, o u t-o f-p o c k e t paym ents for M edicare hospital in ­ surance are to increase 19 per cent on 
Jan. I, meaning that elderly or d is a ­ bled persons entering a hospital will he responsible for the first $124 of their initial bill for a two-month p e r­ iod, up from $1o4 in 1976. 
Premiums hiked 
Monthly prem iu m s for su p p lem en ­ tary m edical in s u ra n c e will he in ­ creased by 5o cents for 25 4 million 
Medicare beneficiaries beginning in 
Ju ly . T h e in c r e a s e w ill r a i s e th e monthly prem ium to $7.7o. 
P ersons receiving Social S ecurity b e n e fits c a n g a in fro m a n o t h e r change this year. The limit on e a rn ­ ings they can m ake without losing the r e t i r e m e n t b e n e f its h a s b een i n ­ creased from $2,76o to $3,ooo.

“ Two

Hill rules on contracts 

AUSTIN 
(AP) 
- 
A school district should not contract with a com pany which employs a m em ber of the d is tric t's board of trustees in a m anagerial position. Atty. Gen. John 
Hill said Friday, 
Hill said it makes no dif­ ference whether the trustee derives “ direct financial benefit from the contract.”

FUNERAL CHAPELS 

( 

Weekend 
Visit n y GEORGE 
C A R M A C K 
P hotos by 
Bonnie* («pni«i*k 

The U.S. Fish & Wildlife 
Service has more than 28 mil­ lion acres of public lands. 

This is more than all the land in the state of Ohio and any one of I ti other states. 
One of its smallest holdings is down on the Rio Grande in Texas. This lit­ tle tract covered with trees and brush 
—and with three sm all lakes — is only 1.98o acres. 
Yet the sign at the entrance calls it: 
“The Gem of the National Wildlife 
Refuge System.” 
And the entrance sign further says: 
“ Subtropical habitat for: 325 bird species, 3o m ammals. 3o reptiles and am phibians, exotic butterflies and moths.” 
Benefits 
And literature on the refuge points out — 
“ Santa Ana benefits more species listed in the B u reau of Sport F i ­ sheries and Wildlife’s ‘Red Book’ of rare and endangered species than any o th e r refu g e in the N ational 
Wildlife Refuge System.” 
But after visiting it, who would want to use statistics in talking about the wonders of Santa Ana Refuge? 
Bonnie and I spent a day recently on Santa Ana Refuge. The refuge is on the Rio G rande about 15 m iles southeast of McAllen. Its entrance is on U.S. 281 directly south of Alamo, 
Tex. 
And there is no better way to tell about Santa Ana than to tell about the 
“ birds who come to dinner” — the strange and beautiful birds who come to feed out in front of one of the re­ fuge’s photographic blinds. 
Wonderful as are the trees — in­ cluding a gnarled giant believed to be the oldest and largest ebony in the 
U.S. —the Spanish moss that even outdoes L ouisiana — the tan g led shru b s and vines — th ere a re 45o species of plants — the little lakes shining in the sun — the cattails — the 1oo-year-old Spanish cemetery — 
The birds 
The thing that m akes Santa Ana truly “The Gem” are the birds. Many are b rig h t-p lu m e d tro p ic a l b ird s from Mexico and even farther south in Latin America. 
I 
The best way to see these birds is through the little narrow slits in the photo blinds — the “ portholes” for the photographer’s lens. 
Each of the green blinds is divided into two p arts and two people can stand in each half. 
Bonnie was never busier — shoot­ ing picture after picture and “ Oh­ ing” and “ Ah-ing” in a whisper after each shot. I stood behind her and m arveled at the birds com ing and going between the thicket and the barren, gray trunk of a long-fallen mesquite in the little clearing in front of the blind. 
Our favorite of all the birds we saw through the "porthole” was the cha­ chalaca. 
The chachalaca gets its name from the calls the birds give, particularly during the mating season. 
The chachalaca is plentiful in many places in Mexico but South Texas is the only place it is found in the United 
States. 
It is a large bird — about the size of a hen. But the head and beak resem ­ ble a dove or pigeon and not a chick­ en. Its color is a sort of gunm etal gray with a faint touch of brown. 
More than any other bird, the cha­ chalaca sym bolizes Santa Ana Re­ fuge. There are more of them here than at any other place and they are typical of the many “ cross the Rio 
Grande” birds that come here. There is only one road in the park, a nar­ row. one-way road that winds 6.7 miles — generally just back from the border of the refuge. 
It is called Chachalaca Road 
Friendly 
The chachalaca is a friendly bird. 
One of the problem s is it dom esti­ cates so easily. But those who came to the blind "spooked” when Bonnie and I made the slightest sound. They would fly off into the brush and then come back one by one with much caution. 
But if the chachalaca is without- 
( color, you should see the green jay! 
It, too, is a tropical bird with an unmatched “ coat of many colors.” 
UH me give you the disjointed notes I made about'the green jay as I stood there in the blind: 
“ Blue head like a blue ja y .. . white triangle under eyes . . bright green back, g re e n er and sh in ier than a parrot. . . going back to tail, back turns a little darker green. . . finally gets a sort of greenish-gray cast. . . then, suddenly, a bright blue tail, just 

S a n t a A n a b e n e f i t s more* r a r e anti e n d a n g e r e d s p e e i e s th a n a n y o t h e r r e fu g e in th e n a t io n a l r e f u g e s y s t e m 

» > 

 like our jays. . . very light greenish- yellow under throat and on breast. . . larger than a blue jay.” 
Most of the time there were only two or three of the green jays out in front of us — usually a pair on an upright limb of the mesquite trunk. 
But what a sight it was when we once counted IO of them! 
Orioles 

Most people would say the m ost beautiful birds were the orioles we saw. We later were told they were 
L ic h te n ste in ’s oriole, s o m e tim e s called the “ altam ira” in Mexico. 

The bird generally was a bright gold with a black throat and light yellow, black and a touch of white on its wings — beautiful. 
There was an exotic variety of dove there — though Bonnie and I are not experts and it might even have been a strange type of pigeon. 
It is la rg e r and fa tte r th a n a m ourning dove. But the thing that gave it such distinction w ere the feathers on its neck and forward part of the back. 
They were purple and blue — ir­ idescent — changing their hues as the sun c a u g h t th e m or as the bird bobbed its head while moving. 
Nature writing 

Bonnie and I started our day at 
Santa Ana by talking with Don Del- nicki, the refuge manager. Delmcki has done m uch w riting on n atu re subjects — both scientific reports and articles for nature magazines. 
He explained to us why many bird hobbyists found Santa Ana so signifi­ cant. 
“ In one sense, birding is a gam e,” he said “ The goal is to try to see every available species in a certain field. 
“There is a North American bird­ 

 ing area that includes the U.S. and 
Canada. Many people hope to sight every species of bird th a t can be found in that area. 
“ Many of the birds that are nor­ mally found in Mexico o r /in Latin 
America appear in the North Ameri­ can area only in South Texas. 
Migrate 

“ I had a friend from Minnesota who came to Santa Ana. Of the first 
IO birds he saw, nine were new to him.” 
Delnicki showed us a list of 32o birds that have been sighted on the 
Santa Ana refuge. Of these more than 
3o can be found in the U.S. only in 
South Texas. 
Many birds migrate through Santa 
Ana. During their spring migration as m any as 2o,ooo b ro ad -w in g ed hawks make a stop at Santa Ana. 
After watching Delnicki band some w hite-fronted doves for study, we took off into the refuge. 
We could not have had a finer guide for the first part of the day. Cruz 
Martinez, an assistant to Delnicki, has been on Santa Ana for 18 years. 
Martinez can identify a bird by on­ ly a glimpse or a single note of his call. 

Spanish ee in elery 

W hat could be more moving than the sight of this old cemetery deep in the brush of the Santo Ana Refuge? The tomb is that of Don Cnstoval leal, buried here in 1876 . But the cemetery was here even before then. 
The fence surrounding it was made of ebony posts. They, too, are believed to have been in place more than a century. 

 martinez nrsi look us to a place that stirred dream s of what life must have been like down on the Rio 
Grande more than a century ago. It was an old, grass and vine-covered cemetery in a brush-ringed clearing. 
In 1834, three Mexican land grants were made here Santa Ana literature reports they were El Agostadero del 
Cato (The Sum m er P asture of the 
Cat), Los Toritos (The Little Bulls) and Santa Ana, from which the re­ fuge gets its name. 
Killed by Indians 

Benigno Leal, the first owner of 
Santa Ana land grant, was killed by the Indians. His ranch headquarters was near where the cemetery now is. 
The cem etery is ringed by great posts, as large around as telephone poles. They are side by side and stick up about six feet above the ground. 
Time has turned them gray — al­ most white. But they are as sound and as sturdy as they were that long ago day when they were put in the ground. 
“These posts are of hand-cut ebony 
— one of the hardest and most lasting of all woods,” Martinez told us. 
Inside the ebony fence, there are a> num ber of grave sites and one ela­ borate brick and plaster tomb The unusual tower on it is about 12 feet high. 
Don Cristoval Leal, son of Benigno 
Leal, was buried in the tom b IOO years ago — Aug. 5, 1876 
From the cemetery Martinez took us lo the nation's largest ebony tree 
Its g re a t tru n k w as k n o tted and twisted and Spanish moss hung from its s p a rs e lim bs It w as grow ing th e re long before .lose E scan d o n brought the first settlers to the Rio 
Grande Valley. 
Largest ebony 

“The tree’s age has been accur­ ately d ete rm in e d ,” M artinez said. 
"It is 3o6 years old.” 
We followed Martinez* tru ck on down Chachalaca Road about a mile, parked and then went along a little path to the bank of the Rio Grande. 
F rom atop a bluff that dropped straight down to the water we looked up the river at a wide and gently curving bend. Red-tinged willows on the Mexican side were a backdrop for the beautiful view of the river. 
Martinez then took us to a photo­ g ra p h ic blind and left us a fte r suggesting we hike several trails. We were in the blind more than an hour 
— a thrilling experience. 
T hen it w as lunch in a p retty clearing set aside for picnickers — followed by two trails Martinez had suggested. 
The first was the Cattail Trail. 
The cattails rn the marshes were a reminder of boyhood How appealing they were — and then we saw the lake 
— shaped like a boomerang and blue and sparkling in the sun 

T a l l i e d 

The white-fronted dove is one of the most beautiful birds. Gener­ ally it is found in Mexico but many make their way to Santa 
Ana refuge. Here Don Delnicki, refuge m anager, bands a white-fronted dove as part of a program studying the bird's mi gration and other habits. 

As we approached the lake we saw a half dozen patches of ducks Two big w ater b irds - w e called them glebes — rose from the water 
There, were perhaps a dozen coots in the water right in front of us and they took off lint most of them did

 not get high enough to keep their feet from trailing in the water What a pretty wake they made 
Little islands of reeds were sc at­ tered about the lake Appealing as they were, they were no more so than the m iniature sunflowers blooming on the dike. 
Bearded trees 

But the trail we will re m e m b er longest was the Owl Trail. 
This is an area of tall trees, be­ whiskered by Spanish moss. Great lieards, 15 feet long, hung from every limb. There was a fat oak with so much moss hanging from it that it looked like a giant white umbrella. 
But even it could not m atch the almost perfect circle of tail trees, all m o ss-co v ered . Rays of sun cut through the white moss, giving the circle the solemn air of a woodland chapel. 
Yes, Santa Ana is not just for the birds. 
Hook-billed kites draw big crowd 

Rare birds have a great fascination for the true bird lover. 
Consider the hook-billed kites that have shown up at Santa Ana National Wildlife Re­ fuge. 
“The hook-billed kite nests in Mexico but is not common even there,” Don Delnicki, refuge manager, says 
“ Records show that two hook-billed kites showed up at Santa Ana in 1964 They were not seen again for ll years. 
“ Then last December four showed up at Santa 
Ana One pair even nested here and two eggs hatched. One of the young birds disappeared 
But tile other r< mauled until it ‘fledged’ — that is grew big enough to leave the nest and fly away. 
“ The last s. gilling oi the hook-lulled kite here w a s last .Inly. 
‘ But during the period the hook-billed kite was here, there were close to 
Kl.(MM) visitors trying to sis* this bird that is so rare in the U.S."

Good old days come back to Mickey Rooney 

By LEE GRANT 
LO S A M O U E S TIMES SERVICE 
"You're either red hot in this business or ice cold. 
One day you don't know what to paw n next; then you re right tin top again." 

Mickey Rooney. 
HOLLYWOOD - Mickey 
Rooney, the Mick, 56 years old, is right on top again. 
The movies are after him 
— two major films recently completed, more on the way 
— a prime-time TV series for Norman Lear debuts next month, and a new and successful night club act has led to lucrative dates in 
Las Vegas. 
These are the good old days for Rooney, “blessed’* days he calls them, long removed from the traumas of bankruptcy and divorce courts. 
He has been through many millions and many marriages (eight of them). 
Mother in law 
“ I haven’t been to Las 
Vegas for three wives,” he said during his Sahara Ho­ tel act recently. 
“I ll also be appearing at halftime of the Rose Bowl game this year — Mickey 
Rooney and his all- m other-in-law m arching band.” 
He is a talented man, a diminutive one (“I was 
6-foot-5 when I married Ava 
Gardner” ), who roamed the MGM lot as a kid for more than a decade with pal Judy Garland and turned out a series of films framed around a character named Andy Hardy. 
‘‘I am not a wealthy man,” said Rooney, “ but 
I’m wealthy in friends, in mv children, my fans and

 the people who’ve stuck by me.” 
Producer-direetor Stan­ ley 
Kramer corralled 
Rooney for “ a sm all but powerful role” in “The Do­ mino P rinciple” which costars Gene Hackman and 
Candice Bergen. 

Rooney plays Hackman's cellmate in the film about a man pressured by an intel­ ligence agency to assassin­ ate the President of the 
United States. 

At Disney 
Next. Rooney found him­ self for the first time at 
Walt Disney Studios for a role alongside Helen Reddy and Shelley Winters in the musical “Pete’s Dragon.” 

In it. he portrays a tip­ pling lighthouse keeper who shares a vision of the dragon with a little boy. 

The film is sparked by a rousing series of song- and-dance numbers with 
Miss Reddy 

Soon Rooney returns to the studio for the animated feature “Fox and Hounds.” in which he assum es the voice of the fox. 
There is a plethora of other Rooney projects — business ones like senior citizens clubs, talent schools for children, commemorative coins — including a new film called 
“Transworld.” 
It is about “an old actor people are trying to de­ fame” and in the cast is flamboyant San Francisco attorney Melvin Belli. 
On stage 
Rooney’s current stage 
[work focuses on a produc­ tion in Chicago of “ ’Three 
Goats and a B lanket,’ a story about alimony,” he said, “ and that certainly fits me.” Rooney plans to turn the play into a motion picture. 

The TV show is “A Year at the Top” and it pre­ mieres on CBS Jan. 19. 

The story is about four old vaudevillians who swap their souls to the devil for one year as a singing group atop the modern recording industry. 

There’s more — screen­ plays (“Piggyback” set in the Salinas area around 
1936 and written for Jack 

Nicholson and 
Sarah 
Miles), novels (“The Pan* ama Canal Incident” about a man demanding $18o million from the United 
States or he’s going to blow up the Panama Canal) and even occasional time for introspection. 
“I’m going to keep work­ ing as long as the public wants me,” said Rooney. “I am very blessed and so very grateful.”

A Yunnan broadcast ap­ pealed for care in attacking officials who rose to prominence under radical influence of the past. It in­ dicated some have been subjected to indiscriminate action without considera­ tion for actual guilt. 

W ould hinder 
Such actions could upset the efforts of Hua Kuo-feng, 
Mao’s successor as party chief, to woo the radicals with a promise of leniency to those who repent and come over to his side.

 strife 

WASHINGTON POST SERVICE 
HONG KONG - Many people have been killed in an “ all-round civil war” waged in China’s most po­ pulous and perhaps most faction-ridden province, and official radio broadcast bas disclosed. 
The broadcast from the huge southwest province of 
Szechwan, monitored here 
Wednesday, indicated that the fighting cried with the 
October purge of four lead­ ing Peking radicals, but called on Szechwanese to 
“ eliminate the remnant poison’’ of those who caused the trouble. 
It was the first clear sign of the severity of recent political strife in Szechwan and seemed to indicate that the province never really settled down after its well- publicized bloody confron­ tation during the Cultural 
Revolution IO years ago.

EXPRESS NEW S CAPITOL HIRE AU 
AUSTIN 
— 
County commissioners courts would have an option in sitting as tax equalization boards under a House reso­ lution pre-filed Friday by 
Bexar County Rep. Hon 
Bird. 
Bird’s House .Joint Reso­ lution (H JR ) 2o proposes amending Art. VHI, Sec­ tion 18 of the Texas Consti­ tution. The legislature must approve of it first by two-thirds vote. 
If later approved by voters, the amendment would give commissioners courts the option of ap­ pointing citizens to the tax board each year instead of themselves. 
“ The amendment would relieve the commissioners of the burden of sitting in judgement on tax disputes if they so choose similar to the appointment of city boards of equalization,’’ 
Bird said. 
Bird’s resolution will be brought before the Texas 
Legislature for considera­ tion after it convenes Jan. ll.

LOS AN GELES (A P) - 
A Superior Court judge refused on Friday to reduce bail for former Charles 
Manson follower Leslie Van 
Houten, who has been granted a new trial. 
Judge Jack E. Goertzen conceded that Miss Van 
Houten had made progress in prison, but he said the 
“ gravity” of the charges required bail to remain at 
$2oo.(MMI 
The former Monrovia, 
Calif., high school homecoming princess, now 
27, goes on trial a second time next month for murder and conspiracy in the deaths of grocery store owner Leno LaBianca and his wife, Rosemary, in I9B9. 
She was not charged in the Manson family slayings of actress Sharon Tate and four other persons the day before the 
LaBianca murders. 
Miss Van Houten’s 1971 conviction was overturned last month by the state 
Court of Appeal

America will miss a gracious First Lady 

By JUDY CLARES 

NEW YORK—Dear Betty: 
You didn't ask to be F irst 
Lady. You didn't actively seek the job, and most of us got the impression you would be .just as happy without it. 

Hilt when it was thrust on you, you were there, doing it up right when we needed you most. 
You were frank, honest, open, na­ tural — all the things we had begun to think First Ladies couldn't be. Hut most of all. you were h u m a n . You mad e us more comfortable with our­ selves 
Oil, we d i d n ’t alw ays a g re e with you. of course . But we couldn't help but love you for the way you were making us feel again. 
While we were being b o m b a r d e d with reports of the inevitable demise of tin* A m e ric a n family, th e r e you were with a doting husband and four h a n d s o m e , w e ll-a djuste d, de vote d. normal children. 
You w e re a s t e a d y in g influence, you with your boys who'd rather ride bucking broncos, plant trees, or go skiing than bask iii the limelight of 
First Family. 
You with a s t rin g y - h a ire d , often barefoot and bluejean-clad dau ghter who’d ra th e r have an a p a r t m e n t of her own than the glamorous prere­ quisites of White House living. 
No f a n c y f i n i s h i n g s c h o o l s , no s n o b b i s h n e s s , no i m p u d e n c e , no phoniness — .lust a regular American family doing things re g u la r A m e r i ­ can families do. 

You were ever the mother. Your boys admitted they had smoked m a r ­ ijuana. You weren't shattered. You may not have approved..but they were no less your sons. 
You admitted — theoretically, of course — — that you would try to u n d e r s t a n d if y o u r d a u g h t e r c o n ­ fessed to having an affair Mothers everywhere knew what you meant. 
We all know we may not always like everything our children will do, but they are our children and we ll love them nonetheless. 
T h a t’s what families are all about. 
You were a welcome re m in d er 
And you were ever the wife. You often kissed and hugged your hu s­ band for all the world to see. It seemed always spontaneous, genuine 
— as if you really liked him. 

I s s u e s 

Yet. you were your own person, often in dis agreem en t with him on weighty issues, always e x p re ssin g your ow ti mind 
You made us feel less like freaks, those of us who also have minds of our own, but who love our men, too. 
Best of all, your husband seemed to like you that way. We got the im p res­ sion he really m eant it when he said he was proud you spoke your mind. 
So there you were, rem in ding us that m ar ria g e s can work in wonderful ways. 
You made us proud. Betty Ford Our hearts were with you as you — at your h u sb a n d ’s request — read his con­ cession statement to the world. 
You were gracious and dignified.

Cops on walks 

Is it legal for the police to drive their three-wheeler scooters on the sidewalk? 
If not, why was a policeman driving on the sidewalk eastbound on Commerce Street near the Convention Center earlier this week? 
It doesn’t teach much respect for the law when tin* police engage in these sort of shenanigans.

No it is not legal, police officials said emphatically. 
Howexer, there are ex ­ ceptions to almost anyth­ ing. 
Generally, police are to abide by all traffic laws unless they are responding to an emergency. 
Police were interested in the episode of driving on the sidewalk, and we pro­ vided them with the time and place but they said they could not track the officer down without the number of his unit. 
We checked back with 

 you and you didn't happen to get it. 
Maybe the officer will volunteer an explanation, but it would seem a bit strange to imagine an> but the most unusual circum­ stances that would require him to drive his three- wheeler down a city sidewalk. 

We do know there was no apparent emergency at that location at that time of the day. As best we can deter­ mine. he shouldn't have been doing it.

San Antonio EXPRESS-NEW S— Saturday January I 1977 
Rest key therapy for a cold 

Dear Dr. Thosteson: You hear and read so much these days about flu and colds. Just how long should a normal cold last, and what does it mean if it lasts longer than that? — Mrs. P.S.p 

A cold is a virus infection of the respiratory tract. There 
’are about a hundred or m ore different viruses, some apparently more stubborn than others. But a “ norm al" cold (no illness is normal) may last from about a week to 
IO days. It is what is called a 
“ self-lim itin g ” infection — that is, it runs its course no matter what we do to treat it. 
All you can do is make your­ self as comfortable as possible. 
If a cold lingers on, you can be fairly certain that another culprit is involved, a bacterial infection, for example, of the sinus or lungs. If a cold is neglected, a person becomes more prone to such secondary infections. T hat’s why rest is important therapy for a cold. 
Hiatal hernia 
D ear Dr. T hosteson: Six m onths ago I had bleed in g from the rectum and went to my gynecologist. X-rays showed nothing wrong. Yet I have to go to the bathroom five or six times a day and I strain. 
The doctor suggested I drink more water and use suppo­ sitories. I have lots of gas. I also have a hiatal hernia. 
Could that have anything to do with my problem? Do you think I have something wrong with my digestive tract? — 
Mrs. PAV. 
Yes — to both of your questions. 
Digestion is not limited to the stomach and intestines alone. It begins in the mouth and goes through several stages all aiong the length of the digestive tract. What interferes with one stage may well interfere with other stages in this very complex process. 
It could he related to your hiatal hernia. For the benefit of other readers, hiatal hernia is an abnormal opening in the d ia p h ra g m at the point w here the gullet passes through to the stomach. The gullet is far removed from the rectum, but subtle effec ts can occur there. For example, if you had pain and difficulty swallowing because of the gullet problem. 
Frequent bowel movements warrant an inspection of the colon by proctoscope. I suggest you see a doctor familiar with colon problems. Perhaps my booklet dealing with hiatal hernia may be helpful to you. For a copy, send 35 cents to me in care of this newspaper. Enclose a stamped, self-addressed envelpe. 
Hypospadis 

Dear Dr. Thosteson: The opening of my penis is on the underside of the penis and not at the end where it’s sup­ posed to bt*. 
I have three boys. One is like me. I also have two grandchildren, one with the sam e condition. Can you explain why this happened? — W.M.W, . 
This is called hypospadias, faulty development of the urethra — which is the canal from the bladder to the urinary opening in the penis. It is passed on through generations, with the frequency of appearance varying. 
It can bt' corrected surgicay, and should be in most cases. Sterility may result from hypospadias, but appar­ ently not in your type. A urologist can advise you about the ideal time for any surgery*. In general, it is best to wait until a child can cooperate to some extent. However, it is thought best to have it done by the third year. In some cases late childhood is the best time. 
Figures I have indicates it occurs in one out of about 9oo births. I ii the female, the parallel abnormality may create a channel from the urethra into the vagina instead of into the urinary opening.

Pick m ade for 
Justice slot 

WASHINGTON (AP) — President-elect Carter and his designated attorney general, Griffin B Bell, have selected federal appeals court judge Wade IL Met rec as solicitor general, sources close to Carter said Friday. 
However, it was not known whether McCree has agreed to leave hts tenured judgeship to accept the appointment 
McCree, 56, has served for IO years on the U.&. Hill 
Circuit Court of Appeals in Cincinnati and previously was a federal judge for five years in Detroit. He is one of only two black federal appeals judges 
McCree’s appointment might blunt some of the cri* 
Deisms voiced by civil rights activists over Carter’s choice of Bell, who has served on the 5th Circuit (burt of Appeals in Atlanta for 15 years. 
Bell has Ihmmi criticized by civil rights groups for some of his judicial decisions, including a 1966 ruling that upheld the Georgia House of Representative's refusal to seat black activist Julian Bond. The U.S. Supreme (burt latf*r unanimously reversed that ruling 
Bell also has been criticized for endorsing fellow judge 
G, Harrold Carswell for the .Supreme Court in 197o, and tor hi^ membership in private Atlanta clubs that exclude blacks and Jews. Bell has said he is resigning from the clubs 
The solicitor general represents the Coiled States in cases before the Supreme Court. The post is the number three job in the Justice Department, below the attorney general and his deputy. 
McCree served with Bell in the late ISHO*, on the Judicial 
Center Board in Washington,

CH D 
Archdiocesan Director has announced. 
Begun in 197o by the Na­ tional Conference of Catho­ lic Bishops, CHD is the 
Catholic church's action/e­ ducation funding program. 

CHD funds projects working for long-term so­ lutions in the critical areas of economic and social de­ velopment, education, health care, legal assis­ tance, communications and housing. 
About one in eight grant 

MSGR. C V. GRAHMANN 
. . . heads CHD campaign. 

 applications is funded; the average grant is $4o,ooo. 
Grants are given to or­ ganizations which are in­ corporated and have an In­ 

 ternal Revenue Service, tax-exempt number. 
Application forms, c ri­ teria and guidelines for the proposals are available at

God must o f new 

There is something deep in the human spirit that yearns for a new start. 
Periodically, we build our symbols for new begin­ nings in the rituals of so­ cieties. 
The primitive, tribal ex­ perience of mankind often center* ‘hem around a feast for new moons or new crop planting. The concept of the new year comes out of this yearning for fresh begin­ nings. 
In ancient Israel Jesus was visited one night by a political and religious leader whose name was Ni- codemus. In the conversa­ tion. Nicodemus asked. 
“ Can a man enter the se­ cond time into his mother’s womb and be horn agaln',” 
The question was neither stupid nor cynical. N'ieode- mus was stating the yearning to begin again and the feeling of hopelessness about that happening. 
He was stating that life operates in time cycles that do not allow us to start over. 
The scars of yesterday s struggles are on us. The

DALLAS (A P ) - The merger between Mercantile 
Texas Corp. and Federated 
Capital Corp. of Houston has been completed, com­ pany officials said Friday. 
Tho merger created the state’s fifth largest bank holding company w ith a to­ tal resources of approx­ imately $2.8 billion, with banks in five of the state's key banking markets.

 options in which we have made our choices have eli­ minated other avenues. We simply have to live with what we have become. 
The Good News of Jesus 
Christ is that it is possible to be horn again. This does not mean traveling back through the time of our yesterdays but does mean that something new and different can be added to our lives. The powerful spirit of God can come to change desire, direction and destiny. 
Born again 
The phrase “ born again” has been sounded with fresh exposure in our secularized world. It has come under considerable examination and some at­ tack during the election of 
Jimmy Carter. It is being debated among religious leaders, lampooned among cynics and witnessed to by a broad spectrum of people from all walks of life as being both valid and essen­ tial. 
It is God s promise to meet the yearning to begin anew. 
Pessimism 
As America enters her third century, the need for spiritually alive people to penetrate the processes of our communities has never been more dramatic. The jaded weariness and pessi- mism that clings to the walls of our society like mustard gas must he aired out. 
A fresh hope must he born. That hope cannot 

 art inning 

 center in the empty tem­ ples of power of political structures. It cannot center in mere resolutions and so­ cieties organized for channeling good will. 
Attitudes 
It must center in the at­ titudes of the inner spirit. It must center in an aware­ ness of a dimension of strength beyond our own. 
There is, however, hope born afresh across our so­ ciety. 
That hope centers in the experience of God at work in our lives to bring :nner harmony to our individual lives and an impact for justice and the righteous­ ness that exalts a nation within our society.

INDIANAPOLIS (AP) - 
Jacqueline' 
Means, a former Roman Catholic who once quit going to church for seven years, will be ordains! Saturday as an 
Episcopalian priest, the first woman to he ordained since the Church author­ ized women priests. 
She jokingly calls herself 
“ God's favorite lady” and wears a 
T-shirt with 
“ Mother Means” emb­ lazoned on the back. 
Mrs. Means has been the target of some opposition from men within the 
Church and a busload of demonstrators is expected at the ordination ceremon­ ies at All Saints’ Episcopal church. 
Two wymen members of the All Saints vestry at the church re­ signed in protest to her or­ dination. 

Wasn’t goal 

Becoming a priest was hardly a lifetime goal for 
Jackie Means, a 4o-year-old mother of four who started attending church again on­ ly for her children's sake. 
She doesn’t even consider herself a religious person 
— at least not in the tradi­ tional sense. 
“ W hats a religious person?” she asks rhetor­ ically. ” 1 don’t think any­ body else knows. But I had a strong belief in God. You know religion is belief in people. Religion lo me is caring about people and belief in other people 
“ The ministry is an ex­ lending of that belief iii people — helping them to help themselves.” 
The daughter of al­ coholic, nonehurch-going parents* she quit high school at lh to elope1 Mrs.

Answer: Two spades. Do not let the excellent spade holding lead you to an over­ bid of three spades. This is still a minimum opening and a single raise is quite enough. 

Moth er o f f o u r will he o rd a in ed 
S a tu r d a y as an 
Episcopalian priest 

Means and her Methodist husband, Delton, were una hie to accept each other’s religion, so they stopped going to church altogether. 
Decided to go 
Seventeen years ago, they decided to go again 
” 1 decided then I hilt we needed to go to church for the kids,” she said “ You don’t ever think you need it. but your kids need it ” 
They chose St. Matthews 
Episcopal church, near their home on Indianapolis' eastside, as a compromise between their conflicting religions. 
That was the springboard for her ministry, and now she’s thinking about becoming a bishop. 
“ That’d be kind of neat,” she said. “ I think the House of 
Bishops, like the priesthood, could benefit from a woman’s viewpoint on things so maybe someday I I I he a bishop someplace Don’t laugh 
People laughed five years ago when I said I wanted to he a priest.” 
Mrs. Means is an easy­ going, good-humored woman who can he shock­ ingly frank. 
She once answered the telephone by saying, “ Hi, this is God” and jokingly warned an employe at the 
Indiana Women’s Prison, where she is a chaplain, 
“ You’re talking to God’s favorite lady now, so you just he careful what you say to me.” 

Her unorthodoxy is re­ flected by her retort to a chaplain who said he felt compelled to tell new in­ mates the “ Good News” — the Gospel. 
“ The only good news these ladies want to hear the first week they’re in this dump is ‘when am I getting out?'” Mrs Means replied. “ If you can’t show them how much God loves them, you can tell them 'til hell freezes over. They’ve heard a lot of talk.” 
Jackie downplays her own role 
“ I can’t do a damn tiling for most of those people, ’ she said. ‘‘I don’t have any miracles that I can work, but just the fact that they sec somebody from the outside coming in that place — talking to them, laughing, joking — means a 
, lot to those people." 

Fuller ministry 

Women priests will give the Church a fuller min­ istry, sin1 said. 
“ I think women are more sensitive in a lot of ways. 
They complement what the men have already been do­ ing because women sci' things differently than men do They pick up things that men don’t.” 
As for male opposition to her ordination, she said: “ I think women do tend to threaten men in all-male situations and I think until you work together, thats not ever going to change 
“ Those guys who will never allow a woman to work with them are miss mg a chance to learn how women function as ‘der* gypeople,’ and learn how to got along. And that’s the only way you can do it.”

3o Argentina leftists killed 

COMPILED PROM WIRE SERVICES 
BUENOS AIRES, Argentina — Gunfire and death ushered out the old year in Argentina 
Friday as security forces reported killing 3o suspected left-wing guerrillas in six different clashes. 
The la te s t k illin g s brought to 1,479 the number of d e a th s for 1976 in the country’s continuing politi­ cal violence. 
This far exceeds the toll from factional w arfare in 
N o rth ern Ire la n d w here 
B ritish a rm y s ta tis tic s show that 296 persons have been slain this year. 
Six g u e r r illa su sp e cts died early Friday in Rosar­ io, A rg e n tin a’s th ird -la r­ gest city located 18o miles north of here, an official communique said. 
M ore th an half of the victims of the violence dur­ ing 1976 w ere reported to have been members of two major Argentine guerrilla groups: the E R P and the 
Montoneros. 
ERP are the Spanish ini­ tials for the People’s Revo­ lu tio n ary A rm y. It is a 
Marxist-leaning organization which believes socialism m u st be in tro ­ duced to Argentina through armed struggle. 

I .S., Saudi 
Trade clim bs 

JEDDAH, Saudi Arabia 
— A m erican tra d e and contracts with Saudi Ara­ bia have nearly doubled in the past year, a U.S. E m ­ bassy official said Friday. 
He confirmed a Saudi re­ port, however, that this oil-rich kin g d o m ’s trade with other countries was growing faster than with the United States. 
The official m ade the statements in commenting on a recent trade report by the 
E n g lish la n g u ag e newspaper Saudi Gazette. 
It claimed U.S. companies had lost billions of dollars to E u ro p e an and Asian co m p etito rs in 1976 and were expected to lose even more in 1977. 

Bishop rejects suggestion 

VATIC AN CITY - A Va­ tican prelate in charge of sacred art Friday rejected suggestions that Michelan­ gelo’s Pieta sculpture and other priceless Vatican art objects be put on sale for funds to aid the needy. 
Bishop Giovanni Fallani, writing in the Holy See’s weekly magazine L’Osser- vato re d ella D o m en ica, said to sell the Vatican’s riches would be tantamount to the triumph of m ateria­ lism over spiritualism. 

Guerrilla gives confession 

BULAWAYO, Rhodesia 
— A black Rhodesian guer­ rilla, in handcuffs and leg irons during a court hear­ ing Friday, said he killed a 
Roman Catholic bishop and two missionaries, all West 
Germans, last Dec. 5. 
Albert Sumbe Ncube, 26, told provincial magistrate 
Jack Riley that he made the statement to police volun­ tarily.

LIMA, Peru — Rescue parties reported all 2(1 per­ sons aboard a DC4 p a s ­ senger plane perished when the aircraft slammed into a mountainside in the Andes of northern Peru, an airline official said Friday. The crash occurred Thursday.

Value.,, still an old acquaintance 

Never forgotten at Morton/Southwest 

When so-called “ experts” claimed that new homes today would have to be built with less to be sold for less, or at more affordable prices, a local building-developing firm those to “ think positive” instead. 
M orton/Southwest never had been a firm to offer the least of anything, and this kind of skepticism was not about to make it begin. 
Took lead 
It's th is school of thought that gave Morton/ 
Southwest a lead in new home sales last year. And what did it give the buyers who chose Morton/South­ west homes? 
The answer is simple and obvious. It gave them what they w anted m ost: th e ir money s worth and more. 
Accordingly. 
Morton/ 
Southwest has initiated a new concept called “ Mon- eysworth.” The firm wants all home hunters to know 
Morton/Southwest homes mean just that: their mon­ ey's worth. 
Morton/Southwest has se­ lected three neighborhoods ascites for furnished, pro­ fessionally decorated m o­ dels to play key roles in its 
Moneysworth campaign. 
The com m unities, each in a different San Antonio area, feature models which show how M o rto n /S o u th ­ west avoided s trip p in g q u ality fe a tu re s from 

 homes and cam e up with lively floor plans. 
These models represent the two most recent series of housing for today's mo­ dern fam ilies: F re ed o m 
Homes and Success Homes. 
Their selected neighbor­ hoods are Cimarron, in the 
U niversal City v icinity; 
Lakeside, a so u th eastern 
Bexar County neighborhood d esig n ed for r e c r e a ti o n - minded families; and Val­ ley Forge in northeastern 
San Antonio. 
Top priority 
Morton/Southwest knows, of co u rse, that everyone w an ts to live w here i f s worth the money, and the firm kept that in mind as a top p rio rity w hen these com m unities were chosen for its F reedom and Suc­ cess Homes 
The firm feels the main reason it outdid the others last y e a r is th at its custom ers know they are living where it's worth their money. 
They have a home w hich suits their individual needs and desires since they got to select it from a variety of market-tested floor plans. 

Thus, home buyers, who had been told they would have to settle for a lot less to move into a new home, discovered this was not so at all. 
Morton/Southwest showed them, they can still get their money’s worth — not only 

 in a new home but in a de­ sirable neighborhood.too. 
F re ed o m 
H om es a re priced from $26,9oo. The three- and four-bedroom series will be modeled in all th ree c o m m u n itie s th is weekend. 
S uccess 
H om es, 

 priced-from $33,6oo, will be m odeled in Valley F orge and Lakeside. This series has rapidly in creased in popularity due to their nu­ merous extras. 
C im a rro n is off K itty 
Hawk Drive just south of 
NE FM 16o4 in Universal 

City. The community has a western flair due to its rus­ tic surroundings. Its resi­ dents have access to a swim club, scout hut and play­ ground. 
In northeastern San An­ tonio, Valley Forge is off 
O 'Connor 
Road and 

 between Nacogdoches Road and NE 11133. R e sid e n ts have the exclusive use of a tennis-sw mim ing facility.

S7 and east of SE Loop 411) 
Amenities here include a lake for boating and fish­ ing. park-like picnic area, sw im m in g pool, lig h ted tennis courts, playground, jogging and biking paths

The Greater San Antonio

Hunch.

•laros/euski said the par­ ade has two major objec­ tives

— to bring before the home buyer and ch surlier new proeucts and concepts in the home products show

Newest, finest 
The Great Northwest is one of San Antonio’s newest 
— and finest communities. 
It’s the kind you’d be proud to call home. proud to call home. 
As if they were carved into the tree-filled environ­ ment are great new homes priced from $35,ooo to 
$48,5oo. 
Builders — Coates Deve­ lopment Co., Carlos Madrid 
& Associates, Royal Crest 
Homes and The Burns Co. 
— are presenting a most impressive display of new homes. 
All include energy-saving features such as 75 per cent masonry, extra insulation. attic ventilation systems and environmental posi­ tioning to take full advan­ tage of the native trees. It’s a great way to save on en­ ergy! 
To enhance these sur­ roundings, there are homes with natural wood and rock exteriors. 
Even the interiors are in­ fluenced by the natural beauty, with huge windows 
Iramtng the picturesque views; window seats for sour quieter, reflective moments; sliding glass doors opening onto shaded 

 patios; and garden kitchens with serve-outs. 
Besides the standard new home features of central air and heat and modern kit­ chens, Great Northwest homes include fireplaces; beamed, cathedral ceilings; exquisite carpeting; sunken areas; two-car garages; and much, much more. 
Because 
The 
Great 
Northwest is out of the realm of city taxes, month­ ly payments here are lower. 
School bus service is pro- vided to the elementary, middle and senior high schools of the excellent 
Northside District. 
As its name applies. The 
Great Northwest is located in the robustly growing sector of northwestern 
Bexar County. 
Less than IO minutes away is the USA A complex and the still-growing South 
Texas Medical Center. 
Even closer is the Univer­ sity of Texas at San Anton­ io. 
The expressway is nearby and puts Lackland and Kelly AFBs juat a short drive away. Wonderland is also convenient for shop­ ping. The next regional mall to be built in San An­ tonio will be nearby.

This Sunday North- cliffe features North- cliffe Country Club 
Estates. 
Northcliffe Country Club 
Estates, priced from 
$8o,75o to $93,5oo, is an ex­ clusive home community surrounded by an 18-hofe championship golf course. 
Some of these homes’ striking features include gourmet kitchens, luxury appointments in master baths, and double fire­ places with a view from the master suite and den. 
Homeowners have a choice of color for paint, carpet, formica and wallpaper, as well as selec­ tion in roof and brick. 
Northcliffe Country Club 
Estates is one section of 
Homecraft’s Northcliffe community, which also in­ cludes Northcliffe and 
Northcliffe East. 
Homecraft offers a number of innovative pro­ grams in Northcliffe, such as its option program and 
"snug home” program. 
The option program is offered in Northcliffe East, and allows a homeowner to add accessories to the basic structure according to his 

 needs and finances. 
The “ snug home” pro­ gram is designed ’ to save money on utility costs, using such features as solar glass tinted windows, special weather stripping, precision-fitted steel doors] insulated foilback sheath­ ing and an energy-efficient heating and air condition­ 

 ing system. 
Northcliffe homes are priced from $37,45o to 
$44,75o, and include three- and four-bedroom models, four floor plans and a choice of traditional, Medi­ terranean and French ex­ teriors. 
Northcliffe East homes include an all-brick exter­ 

 ior, two-car garage, fin­ ished cabinetry, boxed window seats and, in three-bedroom plans, slid­ ing glass patio doors. 
They range from $27,45o to $37,5oo. 
To reach Northcliffe, take NE IH 35 to the Cibo- lo-Hubertus Road exit and follow the signs.

Evaluate home site trees 

Son Antonio EXPRESS-jNlEWS— -Saturday January I 1977 
Pa9e 
5-D 

By TERRY WILBOURN 
County Extension Horticulturist 
Sould trees be removed from residential property before home construction? 
Or should they be saved? 
Shade trees can add thousands of dollars to the value of residential property—yet home-build­ ing contractors often re­ move them before starting construction. Many trees can be saved with little ef­ fort or expense; many are valuable enough to justify considerable effort and ex­ pense in protecting them. 
How can you tell if a tree on your lot should be saved? You will have to evaluate the tree — is it really worth saving? — and you will have to decide what type of protection will be necessary to save it. 
Expense 
How muon will it cost? 
That depends on the amount and type of protec­ tion. There are no shortcuts to good protection; you can’t halfway protect a tree and expect it to survive. 
You can decide which of your trees to save by eva­ luating each one of them carefully. 

First consider their loca­ tion. their species and their size, age and vigor. 
Consider the cost and work involved in protecting them. Consider the location of each tree with respect to where it w ill fit in the 

 landscape when the house is built. 
Ask yourself these ques­ tions about each tree: 
• Will a tree in this lo­ cation provide shade where 
I want it? Or will it shut off the sunlight I need for my lawn and garden? 
• 
W ill it protect my home by breaking the force of cold winter winds? Will it prevent the circulation of cooling summer breezes? 
• Will it hide an unplea­ sant view? Will it block out a desirable view? 
• If no tree were grow ing in this spot, would I plant one here? 
Consider the species of each tree. Is it a desirable species? Is it the kind of tree you would buy? 
Roots 
Do the roots of the tree grow close to the surface? 
Some trees — maple, cot­ tonwood, dogwood and most conifers — often have roots that grow close to the surface of the soil. Lawns and some kinds of orna­ mental shrubs have a hard time surviving under these trees. 
Some trees — willows, for instance — are notorious for blocking sewer lines with their roots. 
Keep these points in mind when you evaluate the size, age and vigor of your trees: 

• Old trees do not adapt as well to changes in envir­ onment as young trees of 

 the same species. Neither, generally, do large trees. 
• A small tree is easily replaced; replacing it is often cheaper than pre­ serving it. 
• A young tree may be the right size for your landscape now. but it may become too large for its lo­ cation by the time it ma­ tures. Often a young tree near a building, walk or driveway will need exten­ sive pruning to keep it in bounds. It may even need to be removed at a later date. 
• The length of annual twig growth, the number of dead branches and the size and color of the leaves are indications of health and vigor. Compare the tree with other trees of the same species. 
Evaluation 
When you have evaluated the trees on your property, you may decide to keep on­ ly a few of them — those

Gardeners’ Questions— Answers 

By TERRY WILBOURN 
County 
Horticulturist 
Somewhere I have heard that floratam St. Augustine grass is resistant to brown patch disease. Is this so? 
No. but it is resistant to St. Augustine Decline (a virus disease) and to chinch bugs. 
I ani seeing tiny black insects flying around my pot plants. It seem s they are coming out of the soil. W hat are uiey? How can I control them? 
Most probably they are fungus gnats, and they do come from the soil. The immature grub or larvae is in the soil. If infestations are heavy, the larvae may injure the plant by feeding on the roots. The adults are just a nuisance. 
Drench pot with diazinon for larvae control. 
What things do I need to be doing to my peach trees now? 
Spray with an oil emulsion for scales, treat for borers and get prepared to do necessary pruning.
