 that are ideally located, are vigorous, have desirable characteristics and require a minimum of protection to save them. You will want to remove those that fall short of these requirements. 
Remove the trees you don’t want and those that are not worth saving Indore the construction work be­ gins. Experienced arbor­ ists, or tree surgeons, can remove them so that the remaining trees will not be damaged. 
The trees that you want to save will have to be pro­ tected from damage result­ ing from one or more of the following: 
• Construction equip­ ment and supplies. 
• 
Grade changes — either raising or lowering. 
• Excavations for sewer and water lines. 
They will also have to be protected during cleanup after the construction of your house is finished.

B> ( LIFFORD BROYLES AND MIKE BRI TON 
C» TH) IXPRtSS NEWS STAN 
That new league and a long-awaited state championship topped sports news in San Antonio during the Bicentennial year. 
Iii a year when history was spotlighted from nearly every conceivable angle. San Antonio made its niche in the record books when the Spurs finally joined the NBA and Churchill completed its drive to the state football crown. 
The drama that culminated in the end of the American 
Basketball Association, the red, white and blue ball and the three-point shot, came to a head on the morning of June 17 in 
Hyannis, Mass. It was a Thursday. 
The National Basketball Association, for a fee of $3.2 million per team, took in San Antonio, Indiana, Denver and the New 
York Nets. A new era in San Antonio had begun. 
Then came Churchill. 
Not even a clear favorite to win its district title when the year began, the Chargers were tougher to budge than taffy before the season was half through. 
Churchill, with a 15-1) season, brought San Antonio its first state championship since Lee's 1971 club, when the Chargers blunted the sharp claws of the Temple Wildcats, 1o*o, on a chilly afternoon in mid-Decemnber. 
It was an early Christmas present for San Antonio's pride. 
Also in 1971) the City Council agreed to enlarge Con­ vention Center Arena by 6,ooo seats to boost the Spurs, 

 while Thunder soccer team took its business elsewhere. 
The Thunder and owner Ward Lay bid SA Aloha and changed their North American Soccer League address to 
Honolulu. 
San Antonio's fate as a soccer city has not yet been sealed 
The Philadelphia Atoms, treading hazardously on an eco­ nomic base, have shown an interest in moving their franchise south. When the new year began there was still no definite word one way or the other. 
San Antonio was host for the Texas League All-Star game, 
Aug. 2. The slumping Texas Rangers came south to bombard the All-Stars, 18-4, before a packed house at V.J. Keefe Field.* 
Others tim es were not as good to the Brewers. 
Owner Jack W illiams, fuming at the lack of support from 
San Antonians, announced he would sell the club. He did. 
Former major leaguer Wally Moon bought it and renamed the team the Dodgers, his old big league ally. Moon then announced he would bring in the Dodgers as the parent club and left the Texas Rangers out in the cold. 
St. Mary’s won yet another Big State Conference basketball championship, but failed in its bid to advance to the NAIA tournament for the third straight year. 
The Rattler baseball team earned a spot in the limelight, advancing to the playoffs and, for the first time in school history, advanced to the NAIA World Series in St. Joseph's, 
Mo. 
Traditional baseball power South San Antonio was at it

Vito captured the Class A All-Events title in the Men’s State tournament in Houston with a 2,o89 total. 
That w asn’t all from the keglers as Mal Meek and Milt 
Syring combined to take fifth place in the regular doubles competition at the national bowling meet in Oklahoma City during the month of May. 
It was indeed a notable year in sports in SA.

Son Antonio EXPRESS-NEWS— Saturday January I 1977 

Suggestions fo r the neiv year 

Okay gang, let's suck it up and try again de­ spite the aching head. Take two aspirins and a 
Hop-plop, phizz-phizz. Go ahead. ITI wait. 
Maybe we’ll all feel better on this first day of ’77. 

Now then, let s forget all about resolutions for the new w ar. My all-tim e record for keeping resolutions is 45. i'hat’s 45 minutes, not days. It’s downright sinful to be so weak 
On the other hand, it’s rath er nice to walk so well with re a lity and recognize my weakness for what it is—a part of me. 
At any rate, since I’m linable to help myself and overcom e such a stout weakness. I come to you on this first day of the new year with great sug­ gestions to help others. 
W ell, would you believe outstanding suggestions? 
Whatever. I like them. 

Suggestion number one deals with the rules of football. Especially pro football. From here it seems that game officials have enough problem s marking the ball on close, crucial plays without being surrounded by wild eyed giants pointing, waving and giving official-type signals. My suggestion is that a team be strapped with a 15-yard unsportsmanlike conduct pen­ alty any and each time a player signals that his side has a tumble, a touchdown, a first down or any other advantage that requires a ruling from the referee. 

The home crowd, usually a capacity of scream ing par­ tisans at any big gam e, is tough enough for officials to 
Confidence can easily slip away 

By now we all realize that the good old USA is about four furlongs behind half a dozen other nations in Olympic competition. It will get worse before it gets better Our government doesn't subsidize our athletes like nearly all the other nations. 
We depend on donations and the pickings have been slim in recent years. T h ere’s no money to pay year-around coaches. Hardly enough to conduct proper try-outs and get the team to the scene. 

So how about this idea? Why not have the IKS. with 

 overcome. The men in striped shirts should not be inti­ midated by some 6-8. 27o-pound water-buffalo pawing at the turf, snorting at the nostrils while holding his paws high to signal a touchdown. 
This is a rule that should have gone into collegiate books long before they made it a penalty for spiking the ball in the end zone. The spiking bit merely took the "hot dog" aet away from touchdown-makers. That cute trick never did have anything to do with settling a contest. 
For that m atter, "assistin g officials” with premature calls also shouldn’t have anything to do with determining issues but more and more fans are beginning to think so. 

High school playoffs 

Okay, if you don’t care for that suggestion let’s try’ another. This one deals with high school football teams that move into the state playoffs. 

Why can’t the high schools borrow a page from the pros and use their season records to determine home teams for playoff gam es? Since Churchill won the state 4A title you hate to look back and find any fault with that brilliant charge toward the title. But if s not just in the 4A ranks. 
Ifs on all levels of high school football. 

Once a team —any team —moves past bi-district the coaches and school officials seem to lose all track of reality. The next game becomes the most important event in the world and all sorts of lengthy negotiations begin with the opposition. 

What I’m getting at, from here it seems durned silly to have fans from both sid e s d rive hundreds of m iles—usually in treacherous winter conditions—to watch a high school playoff game that winds up attracting 8.ooo fans in a stadium that seats 5o.ooo. 

Everyone talks about the gas shortage, the acute economic condition and all that other jazz bat then rival coaches get together and schedule their game 9o miles from the nearest gas pump. Why can’t the team with the best overall season record get the home field? If both teams are unbeaten, or have simlllar 11-1 or 1o-2 records, congressional consent, include one line on each income tax then figure out a method for a tie breaker, using points form . Would you care to contribute one dollar to the scored and points allowed. support of the United States Olympic team ? Then, if so. please add, or su b tra ct, one dollar from your income tax 
You don’t like that suggestion? Okay, we'll move right payment or refund, along to something else. But, before leaving, keep in mind that these high school hassles over where to play are 
L et’s face it, the greatest bill collectors in the urn- nothing new. Thirty years ago I sat in the belly of Alamo verse are IRS officials and approximately 85 million in this 
Stadium for five full hours while officials from Jefferson country file income tax forms each year. If just one-fourth and Lufkin argued over where their state sem ifinal game went for the dollar we’d wind up with millions that we would be played. They wound up in Houston after everyone wouldn't have had for our Olympic team and it would still at the meeting got headaches and missed supper. be an unpressured contribution. 

An O lym pic fund 

Besides, our government just decided to give about IOO million to building ski ramps at Lake Placid for the next winter Olympics and nobody but politicians got to vote on 
... 
. that bill. 
Now we come to my final suggestion for the new year and I like this one best of all. The original idea cam e from 
Ah well, these are just suggestions and not resolutions. 
Clary Drew-, a young man on the KENS-TV production lf you don't happen to care for any of them, happy New 

LOI IE DAMPIER 

By KEVIN O’K E E F F E 
O f IHI tX P R tS S N t w s SI A O 

How does a nine-year veteran of pro basketball lose confidence? It s really not that difficult. 
Spurs’ Louie Dampior can tell you bow, since he is the veteran in question. 
"I didn’t lose confidence in my ability,’’ 
Dampier explained. "B u t I was confused with our offense and was making m istakes earlier in the year. So, I wasn’t playing with any confidence. 
"T h is is the first time I have ever played iii this kind of ail offense. I have always played in a pattern offense with many set plays. I knew how to make moves off those plays. 
"I wasn't getting any shots early in the season because I didn't know where to move off the offense. I was getting laked out a lot on my passes, too 
K now s 
"H ight now*. I ain playing with con fi­ dence. I know what Doug (Spu rs’ Coach 
Moo) expects of me. I am fitting in the offense better, knowing the m oves.’’ 
However, other than some early-season d ifficu lties, D am pier said he hasn t had many problems adjusting to his first year in San Antonio 
" I like the defense we play,” the eight- time ABA all-star guard said. " I like the d efensive concep t h ere. I t ’s ju st m ore overplay. 
"And. unlike the offense, there is a de­ finite pattern to our defense. We do a lot of movement.” 
Dampier is averaging 6.3 points per game, shooting 47 per cent. He is playing about 21 minutes a game 
"I can t complain a whole lot,” he said. 
"M y field goal p ercen tage this year is higher than my career average 
"T h e playing tim e, well, there isn t a 

 player around who d oesn ’t want m ore playing tim e. That s why we are in the league. I don’t think I would be a com ­ plainer if I said I would like to play more. I know I wouldn’t he the only one." 
D am p ier said through p ra c tic e he learned what Moo wanted him to do. 
Shots 

"A t the first of the year, I was taking shots that Doug didn't want me to ," Dam­ pier said. "T h en , I got too conservative, passing up shots that I never ever would have passed up. So. Doug told me to go ahead and shoot.’’ 
After starting throughout his pro career with the Kentucky Colonels and college c a re e r at the U n iversity of K entu cky, 
Dumpier is a reserve with the Spurs But this doesn’t laze the m ustachioed, little man 
"I knew when I cam e down here that I would probably he on the bench,” he ad­ mitted "They had Jam es Silas. Mike Gale, 
George Gervin and George Karl already playing in the hack court. 
"B u t I honestly didn't know if I could come off the bench and play. Heck. I had never done it before. It s really not too difficult. I think I have made the ad ju st­ ment. You are a little stiff right off, but after going up and down the floor a couple of tim e, you are okay.” 

Thoughts 

With 34 gam es down and infinity to go, what are D am pier’s thoughts? 
” 1 have watched our team and we are playing more consistently now,” he said. "W e have lost a few , but they have been close ones. We are playing a lot better as a team. 
"F o r me, it s hard to say. No m atter how much I play, I will give it my best all the time I'm out there.”

Greek picks 
Ags to win 

By JIM M Y (the Greek) SNYDER 
LAS VEGAS — Tomorrow’s Sun Bowl in El Paso pits 
Texas A&M against Florida in a clash of contrasting styles. 
After a midseason turnaround, Texas A&M became one of the most solid offensive and defensive teams in the country-. rolling to six consecutive victories and averaging 
39 points a game to their opponents’ nine — crushing 
Arkansas and Texas on the road. 
Florida h^s the best stable of running backs in the 
South, led by Tony Green, W illie W ilder, Earl Carr, Bob 
Morgan, and Larry Brinson. They also have one of the most dangerous pass receivers in the nation in Wes- 
C’handler, and a Q B-Jini Fisher- who led the SEC in tandem offense witn 1,799 yards. 
But the Gators will be operating against a team that was 
No. 2 in the country- in total defense (No. I against the rush) — hardly the kind of team you make the big play against. And the big play is what Florida has to have to win The Gators’ defense finished dead last in the SEC, giving up an average of 23o yards rushing per game. lf everything goes according to for; n. Texas A&M should win. However, it’ll take at least three touchdowns to do it, because Florida figures to put two TDs and field goal on the scoreboard. 
I make Texas A&M by 8. 
A final look at today’s Big Four howl games: 
Cotton Bowl — Mary land I over Houston. 
Sugar Bowl — Pittsburgh 3 over Georgia. 
Orange bowl — Ohio State 5 over Colorado (down from 
6 ). 
Rose bowl — Michigan 4 over USO (down from 5, and watch for an upset).

DALLAS (AP) - Danc­ ing with “ those who brung us," University of Texas football coach 
D arrell 
Royal and four other sports figures were inducted Fri­ day into the Texas Sports 
Hall of Fame. 
“ Never have so many done so much for just one individual,” the retiring 
Longhorn coach told a lun­ cheon audience attending the traditional prelude to the Cotton Bowl football game. 
A number of former Tex­ as football players were scattered throughout the ballroom and triggered a standing ovation for the 
Winn ingest coach in Longh­ orn history. 
“ Perhaps long after the 

Coogs duel Terps 

Continued from Page IE 

 been given the recognition they are due. But Maryland, the critics say, plays a so-so schedule and competes in a conference (Atlantic Coast) that is rich only in basketball tradition. 
“ Everyone says the ACC is a bas­ ketball conference,” says Maryland fullback Tim Wilson. “ Even though we’ve proved to be a good football team — and our stats show it — we still have to go out and prove it over and over because people think of the 
ACC only as a basketball confer­ ence." 
Says Vince Kinney, the Terps’ wide receiver: “ A lot of people still don’t believe in University of Maryland football, even though we are 11-o and in the Cotton Bowl. And they won’t believe until we go out there on na­ tional TV and show just w hat we can . 
"This game can be a stepping stone for not only our team but also for football in the East.” 
A victory — an impressive one at that — could also mean a national championship for the Terrapins. 

The forecast for Saturday’s Cotton 
Bowl calls for temperatures in the mid-2os and a 3o per cent chance of snow. 

Should top-ranked Pittsburgh lose to Georgia in the Sugar Bowl Satur­ day, M aryland would be the only undefeated major college team in the nation. The Terrapins are rated fifth, following Pitt, Michigan, Southern 
California and Georgia. 
“ If we can beat Houston and go 
12-o, and Pitt gets beat, then we should be No. I,” says Mark Manges, the Maryland quarterback. 
Like Maryland, Houston has an identity problem, although not as great it seems as the Terrapins’. 
Until this year, Houston was a team that — no matter how well it did — was always overshadowed by its cou­ sins in the Southwest Conference. 
But the Cougars solved that prob­ lem for the most part in 1978, going 
9-2 and winning a share of the cham­ pionship in their first year in the 
SWC. 

And a victory over M aryland should erase any remaining doubts about the Cougars. 
This will be Houston’s seventh bowl appearance. For M aryland, its eighth. And it will be the first meet­ ing ever between the teams. 
Offensively, both teams have a lot in common. Houston averaged 28.5 points per game during the regular season, while Maryland scored at a 
24.8 clip. 
In total yards, Houston gained 4,555 yards, Maryland 4,o92. Most of that yardage came on the ground. Hous­ ton piled up 2,988 yards rushing and 
Maryland 2,874. 
Alois Blackwell is Houston’s lead­ ing rusher with 934 yards for a 8.2 average. He has eight touchdowns. 
Maryland, meanwhile, likes to give the football to one of three backs, all of whom have more than 6oo yards rushing. 
Tailbacks Preacher Maddox (678) and Steve Atkins (621) and fullback 
Wilson (61o) have accounted for 
1,9o9 or Maryland’s rushing game.

A gs appear ready 

Continued from Page IE advantage was obvious at Friday’s 
Sun Carnival Luncheon honoring the two teams. 
The program was officially started with a prayer. 
An elderly El Paso gentlem an warmed the big crowd with some smooth, touching words of religion. 
Then came the final statement of his invocation. 

“ Finally, O Lord,” he said, paus­ ing several seconds before continuing 
“ . . . go get ‘em, Aggies!" 
Most of those in attendance roared with approval. 
It was as loose as a goose here 
Friday, some 48 hours prior to Sun­ day’s 2 p.m. kickoff. 
Opposing players enjoyed friendly chats and informal joking at the lun­ cheon. They certainly didn’t look like two teams which plan to knock the bejeebers out of each other come 
Sunday. 
Some of the Florida players got on stage and danced, doing what is known as “The Walk.” 
Aggie Zach Gutherie demonstrated the “ neck Nibbler" on a pretty El 
Paso chick — at A&M he is known as the w orld's greatest lover, they claim. 
Big Billy Lemons of A&M stunned the audience with some fancy sing­ ing, a soul version of “ Amen, 
Amen". 
It was time for fun and enjoyment and many of El Paso’s top names 

 were on hand to take part in the fes­ tivities. 

Lee Trevino invited the two coaches 
— Emory Bollard of A&M and Doug 
Dickey of Florida — to play nine holes of golf, and then entertained the writers covering the game at a cock­ tail party at his new golf club. 
Among the special guests at the luncheon was the mayor of El Paso, who naturally had to put in a plug for his city. 
Mae West 

“ People visiting our city this week have learned that El Paso is sort of like what Mae West said about sex," said the mayor. 
“ When it’s good, if s real good. 
When it’s bad, well, it ain’t all that bad.’” 
The teams and several Florida and 
Texas writers arrived here earlier this week and have been treated to a relaxed fun atmosphere. 
Many of the people here call their annual event the “ Fun Bowl.” 
Dogs 

All has not been fun for the teams, though. 
The Aggie players, for instance, sashayed across the border to the dog track two nights ago and they claim they lost a bundle. 
“ We need to win the game,” joked 
Bollard, “ because we haven’t won so far." 

Chances are, though, that the Ga­ tors won’t be as tough as the dogs for the big and talented Aggies. Bookies say A&M should win by as many as eight or nine points. 

Florida Coach Dickey is hoping it’s that close. 
“ Right now,” he moaned to re­ porters Friday, “ I don’t think there is a better team in the nation right now than A&M.” 
What do you think about that. 
Coach Bel lard’1 
Sincere 
“ I am very complimented and I know that Doug is a very sincere person,’* said Bollard. 
“ But a lot of times you tend to look a lot better before the game than you are when it comes time to play." 
A&M had its last workout Friday. 
“The guys are tired of working out, no doubt,” said Bellard, who quickly added, “ because I am tired of work­ ing out. It felt good when it ended today.” 
Dickey will end his practices Sa­ turday, while most of the people of this city will flood the downtown streets for the annual Sun Carnival 
Parade. 
The Aggies probably enjoyed New 
Y ear’s Eve more than the Gator players. The Gators had a midnight curfew while A&M’s players were free until I a.m. Saturday. 
And luckily, the Aggies were not losers at the dog track Friday. 
The track was closed.

Touchdown

Concern

At home. I have to believe that our own lawmakers and administrators w ill do what they think is best for 
America.

 partments that deal directly with the motor vehicle industry. 
• 
A danger of shutdown of car production in the 1978 model year unless Congress amends the Clean 
A ir Act of 197o and establishes emis­ sion standards that are technologi­ cally feasible. 
• 
A solution to the problem of warranties on emission equipment.

Motoring 
World 
Tile ultimate motor home 

GMC’s new 2 6 -fo o t Kingsley m otor hom e combines excellent ride and handling characteristics w ith m any interior features 
1977 looms uncertain 

By JIM ABBOTT 

AUTO EDITOR 
There is a new luxury motor home out on the nation’s highways called the Kingsley. The new 26-foot motor home is produced by 
GMG Truck and Coach division. 
The new Kingsley, which joins the 
Elcganza ll and Palm Beach models, features simulated wood paneling, russet color nylon seating w ith matching deep-cut pile carpeting and luxurious, fully lined draperies 
For 1977. the Kingsley and all other 
GMF m otor homes feature a new saddle color instrument panel with a more convenient location for the ash tray and various controls. The new models also are equipped w ith a more efficient automotive air condi­ tioner and heating system. 
Floor plan

Available for the first time iii 1977 is tin' new tw in-lied arrangem ent with side bath and seperate shower. 
In te rio r craftsm anship is h ig h ­ lighted by such features a s hand- sanded cabinet drawers fo r added strength, drawers are of dovetail construction w ith glued rather than stapled joints and include dust shields. 

Sleeping ureas ran be a i ra ngeil to a c c u m - analgic six ailalls.

San Antonio EXPRESS-NEWS— Saturday January I 197 7 
Auto industry uncertain 

Continued from Page I dered to meet in the years ahead - especially the 27.5 average miles per gallon figure for 1985. 
The legislation calling for a steady annual improvement in fuel economy is based on the average of the total output of any one manufacturer. 
Certainly, the events of the last tun years have provided the industry with all the incentive it will ever need to turn out products that deliver bet­ ter gas mileage. 
This is true not only of domestic producers but import car builders as well. Since the price of gasoline has risen sharply in the last two years and threatens to go even higher, there is no doubt that the consumer wants all the miles per gallon that he can get. 
At this period in time it is difficult to convince the public that automotive production lines might be stilled in the 197.8 model year. 
The 94th Congress adjourned with­ out laking action on an amendment that would have extended 1977 model year emission requirements for one year. 
The automakers are now testing cars for federal certification based on 
1977 standards. 

7 believe our latvmaliers an d adm inistrators trill do alia! they believe is best for A m erica' 

 lf the 95th Congress does not act, the industry would be in violation if it tried to market any cars built to 1977 requirements. 
What the industry really wants is an amendment to extend 1977 stan­ dards for at least three years 
One problem that has been consis­ tent for several years is a legislative lack of understanding of lead time — the period in which a new car is con­ ceived and brought to market. The span is a minimum of three years. 
P ro g re s s 
Charges that the industry is against clean air are nonsense. 
No sector of the economy has spent more or made more progress in this area than the auto industry. 
Even if no changes were made in the standards that apply to 1977 cars, automobiles still will meet the na­ tional air quality standards set by the 
Environmental Protection Agency for most parts of the country by 1985 
Los Angeles is a notable exception 

Autotoofivi M JH * V~ 

 but California standards consistently have been much more strict. 
To help maintain clean air stan­ dards, it is important that the mo­ toring public does its part by keeping emission equipment in good working order. 
For example, what about, the war­ ranty on such equipment? 
The replacement and repair in­ dustry — a vital portion in the total automotive segment — contends that the five year/5ft,ool) mile warranty would place an impossible strain on service facilities. 
Chis view is shared by the Ameri­ can Automobile Association. l'he 
Automotive 
Information 
Council, an organization of some HOH members with divergent views, be­ lieves that Congress should resolve the problem. 
What the industry has been doing and will continue to do is strive to attain both goals of greater fuel economy and cleaner air. 
Periodic and proper service is im­ portant. 
While automotive service opera­ tions have been the target of consi­ derable criticism, the record shows that the great majority of service outlets are managed by honest and efficient people. 

IN ADDITION to recreational use, the new 19 7 7 Kingsley motor nome can serve as a mobile office for business or industry 
1977 KINGSLEY MOTOR HOME 

AUTOMOTIVt DISPLAY 

Continued from Page I tors homes is the front wheel drive system. 
The front drive plus the tandem independent rear air suspension, low center of gravity, six wheel braking system and aluminum and molded fiberglass body, make the Kingsley one of the nicest riding motor homes 

 on the road. 
Beginning in 1977, the vehicle will be powered by a 4o3 cubic inch V-8 engine, combined with an oil-cooled three speed automatic transmission. 
Extra features that can be added include dual roof mounted air condi­ tioning, AM/FM radio with tapedeck, power campsite leveling device. 

 cruise control and 6,ooo watt motor generator. 
The Kingsley is just about the ulti­ mate in in motor home transportation available today, but then it should be. for what it cost you could buy a pretty nice three bedroom home. 
Of course you couldn't verv well take a trip in a regular home.

FACES IN THE NEW S—remarkable men and women who. for better or worse, cap­ tured the world s attention and imagination 
Some burst briefly and powerfully into the news, but soon slipped from sight Some influenced the way we think, feel, and live 
Some changed the course of history When you remember their faces, you can t help feeling affection, or wonder, or rage, or laughter, or sadness FDR, Churchill. Hitler, 
Ghandi, Einstein, Freud Brando. Chaplin, 
Monroe Would you still recognize Dizzy 
Dean. Dag Hammarskjold, Gertrude Stem? 
And how about Charles Lindbergh, the Dionne quintuplets, even 
Al Capone? 
You ll find them all in this excit­ ing collection of TIME covers — reproductions of 2,8 1o covers from the very first issue in 1923 
This strikingly handsome 56-page portfolio is offered to you free with your TIME subscription
